<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">10.iv.a Class Field Initializers: this semantics</h2><a id="user-content-10iva-class-field-initializers-this-semantics" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: 10.iv.a Class Field Initializers: this semantics" href="#10iva-class-field-initializers-this-semantics"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Jeff Morrison</p>
<p dir="auto"><a href="https://github.com/jeffmo/es-class-public-fields">https://github.com/jeffmo/es-class-public-fields</a></p>
<p dir="auto">JM: main issues</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>
<p dir="auto">Contextual things in the initializer scope.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p dir="auto">Initializers execute at the time of the last</p>
</li>
<li>
<p dir="auto">static field initializers are executed as the last step of class definition evaluation, need access to the class name binding itself.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">(discussion of the scope of initializers)</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: What should be available inside the initializer?</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Banning arguments, new.target (because unclear) -- these are static semantics errors when occurring literally, and <code>undefined</code> in direct eval</li>
<li>Allowing this and super property access</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">YK: It would be confusing to have the old enclosing copies of new.target or arguments inside of the initializers, which run later</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: Can't you write "arguments" in computed properties?</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: We discussed this in the context of the cross-cutting class proposal with Brian</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>You might want this in a static property to refer to the class you just created</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">JM: There have been issues created in the Babel tracker about the scoping of this</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: In a static method this refers to the constructor function</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>The constructor function that the static property is defined on</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">AWB: A consistency for static initializers for the left and right hand side of initializers would be to be "the same as in a static body"</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: open q: "Do we permit or ban new.target or arguments in the right hand side of initializers?"</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: This becomes tricky because some people expect that a field referecnes ambient bindings, but it's easy to get confused if you treat the initializer as a method</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: These initializers are very shorthand for a function, which always has new.target</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: In favor of banning</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: A use case is a static method on subclasses that filters the initializers, as called from a superclass constructor. In that case, you want to call |new.target.initializerFilter()|, and so want new.target to work</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: Even though you can construct models which are consistent about which is which with new.target and arguments, it's equally hard to construct one that is unambiguously clear which one you are referring to</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: This isn't a catchall; there will still be imperative cases that benefit from being written in the constructor</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: If we ban them now, we can always add them later.</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: We also need to decide what to do with eval('new.target')</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: Presumably if it's an early error without eval, then it will throw early during the eval as well</p>
<p dir="auto">(discussion of alternatives)</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: Because this is a convenience tool, we can ban eval without loss of expressiveness. It's just a loss of convenience</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: On instinct, this scares me. Just because we can't come up with a use case, doesn't mean that something won't ever come up</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: How deep would the direct eval ban go? Expression, subexpressions, within arrow functions inside subexpressions, ...?</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: banning direct eval is hard, because it's undecidable whether it's direct eval</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: Yeah, we'd have to ban the entire class of things that could wind up being direct eval</p>
<p dir="auto">MM; The only reason we're allowing these outside of the constructor at all, is for common well-behaved cases</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: Think of this as shorthand for function. If it was a function, all functions define new.target. When invoked you get undefined. Functions have arguments. There are no functions. If you just treat an initializer like an anonymous function, then you get exactly everything falling out</p>
<p dir="auto">DE: If you treat it like just a method, then you get everything you want</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: This avoids a laundry list of special cases</p>
<p dir="auto">KG: What about statics? The easy corollary is that for statics, it's an anonymous static method.</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: This also allows for sane semantics for <code>this</code></p>
<p dir="auto">KG: (whiteboard)</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-js notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="class Base {
  prop = new.target.filter ? new.target.filter(val) : val;
}

class Derived extends Base {
  static filter() {}
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">class</span> <span class="pl-v">Base</span> <span class="pl-kos">{</span>
  <span class="pl-c1">prop</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-k">new</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-k">target</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-c1">filter</span> ? <span class="pl-k">new</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-k">target</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-en">filter</span><span class="pl-kos">(</span><span class="pl-s1">val</span><span class="pl-kos">)</span> : <span class="pl-s1">val</span><span class="pl-kos">;</span>
<span class="pl-kos">}</span>

<span class="pl-k">class</span> <span class="pl-v">Derived</span> <span class="pl-k">extends</span> <span class="pl-v">Base</span> <span class="pl-kos">{</span>
  <span class="pl-k">static</span> <span class="pl-en">filter</span><span class="pl-kos">(</span><span class="pl-kos">)</span> <span class="pl-kos">{</span><span class="pl-kos">}</span>
<span class="pl-kos">}</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">AK: Does this restrict implementation's ablity to optimize away? No, we should be able to statically determine whether eval or arguments are possible.</p>
<p dir="auto">EF: This is a simple front-end optimization.</p>
<p dir="auto">Consensus: Initializers run with the semantics of individual anonymous methods with no arguments being run on the class. That explains the scope of this and super property access; new.target would be undefined, and each would get a new empty arguments object.</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: Do properties get created with DefinePropertyOrThrow() or Set()?</p>
<p dir="auto">(whiteboard)</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-js notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="class FooText extends TextNode {
  textValue = &quot;asdlsk&quot;;
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">class</span> <span class="pl-v">FooText</span> <span class="pl-k">extends</span> <span class="pl-v">TextNode</span> <span class="pl-kos">{</span>
  <span class="pl-c1">textValue</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s">"asdlsk"</span><span class="pl-kos">;</span>
<span class="pl-kos">}</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">YK: Users would likely expect to get a Set; most users don't understand setters, and would expect that setters are always called</p>
<p dir="auto">WH, MM: Would be really surprised by a Set in this example. The intuition is for initializers to use define.</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: You could phrase this either way; either it's a hazard, or it sets the expectation that users can depend on a predictable DefinePropertyOrThrow.</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: The reason why Set doesn't work is that it's not extensible to const, to typed properties, all of these can only be done on DefinePropertyOrThrow</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-js notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="textValue = &quot;adadad&quot;"><pre><span class="pl-s1">textValue</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s">"adadad"</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">^^^ Authors don't immediately recognize that the difference between Set() and DefinePropertyOrThrow()</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: Can't use Set() to define const or typed properties. Only DefinePropertyOrThrow will work.</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: A frozen data property, overriding with Set() will throw</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: Having spent some time on this, I think neither is more intuitive than the other. People are very split on this. So as an argument for which is more intuitive, that's a wishy argument. We should go based on what is more useful.</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: If you define a method named textValue, you can't</p>
<p dir="auto">BE: = looks like Set</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: So we should use colon</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: Colon is reserved for type annotations</p>
<p dir="auto">BE/WH: the only robust semantics is DefinePropertyOrThrow()</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: Only DefinePropertyOrThrow will work across future extensions, for const properties and typed properties</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>evaluate the type, around time of initializer, can't create it and later change type, so it MUST be created with DefinePropertyOrThrow()</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">SP: How is this conflated?</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: The idea that an initializer does a Set(), but a const does a DefinePropertyOrThrow() is just (confusing?)</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: We already have in ES6 practice for how to initialize objects imperatively, with <code>this.textValue = "asdf"</code> inside the constructor. That's imperative, that does a Set(), it has all the properties that Yehuda is advocating. Any proposal that adds new syntax should, to justify the cost of the new syntax, have less overlap with something you can easily say already.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-js notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="this.textValue = &quot;...&quot;;"><pre><span class="pl-smi">this</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-c1">textValue</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s">"..."</span><span class="pl-kos">;</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">...is a Set()</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: This is shocking. That means that you are saying we shouldn't have symmetry</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: I value symmetry, but I don't see a symmetry argument. Leaving aside BE's point about the token, for object literals, all properties in object literals are initialized with DefinePropertyOrThrow</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: But most people will have the imperfect but good-enough mental model to view this as a desugaring to this.textValue = ...</p>
<p dir="auto">BE: This isn't true, as object literals already do something different compared to manipulating the object, if you put a setter on Object.prototype</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: But that is suprising to many users</p>
<p dir="auto">AK: What about developers of other languages who define fields and expect those to not trigger setters (as in other languages, where setters don't happen when you declare fields)</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: This is just like an object literal. Writing object literals has the same problem.</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: That's different because object literals don't have superclasses. People don't use <code>__proto__</code>: very much</p>
<p dir="auto">BE: What's the robust, future-proof semantics? Seems like DefinePropertyOrThrow, as Waldemar says.</p>
<p dir="auto">DE: If you subclass an HTMLElement and try to define an onclick, that would be fired either</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: What about :=</p>
<p dir="auto">SM: Having setters already be there is one use case. The primary use case is turning an existing property into a setter or getter, to shadow something else or make something lazy. If that's the upgrade path, then this will break.</p>
<p dir="auto">DD: That's a good point; people do deprecations by adding setters or getters, and that would fail because it would just shadow</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: If the semantics of this is Set, I would ask, what is the added value of the proposal as a whole such that the proposal pulls its weight. You could get these semantics just by writing the right lines in the constructor.</p>
<p dir="auto">DE: Good from an optimizability standpoint. We've been encouraging people to be explicit about properties on <code>this</code> by adding them all in the constructor, assigning <code>undefined</code> if necessary. This is a nice syntax for that.</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: Using Set() is dangerous, if derived class has foo, exposes you to a super class that didn't have accessor foo, which is later added.</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: Lots of classes which are designed to be subclassed assume that they can intercept Set()s</p>
<p dir="auto">DT: Initialization in C#, Java, etc is explicitly setting up the world and invariants aren't there</p>
<p dir="auto">DD: For us it's different, because super has already returned, etc</p>
<p dir="auto">DT: Using DefinePropertyOrThrow is good because it'll give us that capability from other languages, where you add the properties without invoking setters.</p>
<p dir="auto">SP: In the current proposal (with Set), it's the same as doing the code in the constructor, so the constrained DSL is nice because it is not so order-dependent, right?</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: Sounds like we won't resolve the issue in this meeting; I'll work with some people offline to come to more of a conclusion on this.</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: The last open question: For fields that do not have an initializer, these implicitly initialize to undefined, not copying this.x = this.x as previously. I went back and forth on this; one of the reasons I was against this is that I was concerned about shadowing the same-named property on the parent class. But what convinced me is that, presumably, if you put that declaration there without an initializer, you would intend to initialize it later at some point, in which case you would be shadowing the parent property anyway. So the expectation is that, when you put the field there, you will be shadowing the field above it. So the initialization happening in order makes sense.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-js notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="class FooText extends TextNode {
  textValue; // textValue = undefined;
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">class</span> <span class="pl-v">FooText</span> <span class="pl-k">extends</span> <span class="pl-v">TextNode</span> <span class="pl-kos">{</span>
  <span class="pl-c1">textValue</span><span class="pl-kos">;</span> <span class="pl-c">// textValue = undefined;</span>
<span class="pl-kos">}</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">WH, AWB: Good.</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: In lexical scoping, we decided that the same name in the same scope would always refer to the same binding. The analogue here points in this direction as well.</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: Cases where it's useful to define the value of a child in terms of the parent property value...</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-js notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="this.x = this.x + 1;"><pre><span class="pl-smi">this</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-c1">x</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-smi">this</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-c1">x</span> <span class="pl-c1">+</span> <span class="pl-c1">1</span><span class="pl-kos">;</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">AWB: Suggest creating the fields first, presumably undefined, then assign.</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: Separating creation from initialization is not future compatible with const or typed properties either unless we want to do something equivalent of a TDZ for properties, which I don't.</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: Back to JM's original question, the only sensible thing for a field declaration without an initializer to do is to create the field with the initial value undefined.</p>
<p dir="auto">DD: left hand side of computed property evaluates once (for definition), the right hand side many times (for instantiation)</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-js notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="[this.counter] = this.counter++;"><pre><span class="pl-kos">[</span><span class="pl-smi">this</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-c1">counter</span><span class="pl-kos">]</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-smi">this</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-c1">counter</span><span class="pl-c1">++</span><span class="pl-kos">;</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">JM: concern about syntax, further syntax might help solve, will consider</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB:</p>
<p dir="auto">DD: It should be consistent with fields</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: I see the analogy with methods as being stronger. Anyway, most use cases will be with Symbols.</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: Want consistency across the whole construct, whether method, field or getter/setter</p>
<p dir="auto">AK: This isn't thought of as a statement, but rather as a declaration, so the evaluation of the computed property names goes with the other declarations</p>
<p dir="auto">(discussion re: transpilers)</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: "identifier : identifier" currently means type annotation</p>
<p dir="auto">with respect to this ^^ <a href="https://tc39.es/ECMA-262/#sec-forbidden-extensions" rel="nofollow">https://tc39.es/ECMA-262/#sec-forbidden-extensions</a>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p dir="auto">The Syntactic Grammar must not be extended in any manner that allows the token : to immediate follow source text that matches the BindingIdentifier nonterminal symbol.</p>
</blockquote>
<p dir="auto">JHD: Babel is doing fine with Set, this scoping, etc, and we should take that as a datapoint. No user complaints.</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: Should the properties be defined as configurable or non-configurable?</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: Currently it creates a non-configurable property with DefinePropertyOrThrow</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: Seems strange why we would do it differently from methods</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: I strongly prefer non-configurable. We already have an imperative syntax for imperative initialization, properties that are configurable and disappear. The time-dependent nature of configurable properties don't give you as many guarantees. Non-configurable properties let you minimize gratuitious differences with private state and get static analyzability and static shape. The behavior of private fields is more like a non-configurable property than anything else.</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: I support your position, but in addition: one of the advantages of this new syntax, is that it allows to declaratively define the shape of the object.</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Value in readability</li>
<li>Make non-configurable is a powerful value</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">AWB: what happens when a super class has already defined one of these? When they are configurable?</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: Silent replacement</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Prefer: two declarations of the same own property, an error.</li>
<li>Set() alone doesn't produce an error, DefinePropertyOrThrow() alone doesn't produce an error.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">DE: super constructor might return a Proxy</p>
<p dir="auto">EFT/AK: Implementors can't infer more about shape than before</p>
<p dir="auto">JM: Objections to Stage 2?</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: I want to see this and private fields merged. I don't think we should advance either on their own to stage 3.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h4 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Conclusion/Resolution</h4><a id="user-content-conclusionresolution-8" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Conclusion/Resolution" href="#conclusionresolution-8"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Stage 2 acceptance</li>
<li>Initializers run with the semantics of individual anonymous methods with no arguments being run on the class. That explains the scope of this and super property access; new.target would be undefined, and each would get a new empty arguments object.</li>
<li>No consensus on Set() vs. DefinePropertyOrThrow()</li>
<li>A field with no initializer should create the property with the value undefined</li>
<li>No consensus on configurable vs non-configurable</li>
<li>Revisit initializer token choice of =</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">10.iii.c <a href="https://github.com/benjamn/reify/blob/master/PROPOSAL.md">Nested import declaration</a></h2><a id="user-content-10iiic-nested-import-declaration" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: 10.iii.c Nested import declaration" href="#10iiic-nested-import-declaration"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Ben Newman</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Potentially relaxing restriction that <code>import</code> can only appear at the top level.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: proposal would allow <code>import</code>s that are nestable; hoisted (declarative over imperative); lexically scoped; sync eval'd; back compat.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: We have been shipping this to the Meteor community in a transpiler context. The semantics are more subtle than you would expect to allow, due to the subtleties of modules. They are synchronous (hoisted to the top of the scope) and backwards compatible.</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: [clarifying that bindings are immutable on the import side]</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: [clarifying that currently proposal is transpiled to commonJS require]</p>
<p dir="auto">... discussion about the spec; request to skip the spec discussion at this stage ...</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: How about we consider these to be ordinary lexically scoped variables?</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: If I sit inside a loop, importing a module mutiple times, will I get the same module namespace object?</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Yes. In subsequent iterations, it doesn't get imported again</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: Is the module specifier still restricted to only being a string literal?</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Yes.</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: In this proposal, no matter what, all imports will be loaded beforehand. It's only that evaluation will be deferred until it reaches that point in the code.</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: Do you hoist evaluation as well as instantiation? - I would expect hoisting, for consistency with top-level imports</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: the ability to have nested imports gives you a tool to control relative execution of import declarations to other statements</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: Yes, that's the Optimistic Imports example from the proposal.</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: DH's point is important. If you want predictability, you do not want interleaving.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Modules get all loaded before the program starts. Their instantiations are done as their blocks are encountered in the program.</p>
<p dir="auto">WH: So then what happens if a module is missing when being loaded? How do Optimistic Imports work?</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: necessarily an error if it fails to load.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: ... talking about a use case for cross-env code with conditional imports ... if it turns out that the fetching of the module source fails, it is entirely conceivable that the dev knows this was a possibility and that that import is inside a conditional block that prevents it from evaluating - so I think it's less fatal for the fetch to fail.</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: an important use case for this is "if I'm in env X, import stuff for env X"</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: proposal: we unconditionally do all the fetches, but if any fail, we don't actually fail - just hang onto the error for later. and only if control flow reaches the point where it's needed, does it end up being an error. iow, if you only have conditional imports, failures will not create a compile-time error, and only create a runtime error when the module is needed and missing.</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: consistent with the current spec.</p>
<p dir="auto">AK: changing what used to be early errors into not-early errors.</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: I mean consistent in that it treats each block as if it's its own module.</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: ?? conditional importing means the dev knows it's benign if it's missing, and can work around that.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: ... explaining "isolated scopes" example ... ability to reuse identifiers in the same file, but in separate scopes. With top-level, you need slight identifier variations.</p>
<p dir="auto">SP: also refactoring hazard - if the imports are in the describe, it's easier to split up large files without breaking things.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: in top-level model you still have to do all the same fetching</p>
<p dir="auto">CP: at what point in this program is the error triggered? we intentionally chose declarative import evaluation as well as initialization/loading.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: the specific "it" test in my example will fail if the import nested inside it fails to load.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: it should fail every time its import is attempted</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: it should be possible via the registry to clear things out and make it possible to retry, but only in a staged way - ie, you have to kick off an evaluation, not in the middle of a module.</p>
<p dir="auto">BF: in node, if it evaluates, it's put into the cache; if it fails to evaluate, it's not put into the cache.</p>
<p dir="auto">... discussion about entire dep graph being resolved before any evaluation ...</p>
<p dir="auto">DT: ... general argument that nested imports would have to be VERY compelling to make it worth it ...</p>
<p dir="auto">AWB: what about imperatively using a dynamic import API?</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: loader API does not let you statically express dependencies, so we talked about a syntax that allows dynamic async importing</p>
<p dir="auto">... more discussion ?? ...</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: inherent conflict with top-level await</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: I think the dynamic syntax you're proposing is essential for top-level await. what would happens with current <code>import</code> if you pull in a module using top-level <code>await</code>?</p>
<p dir="auto">CP: top-level <code>import</code> would handle it</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: essentially forks the world - ie, "modules that can use top-level await" and "modules that can't"</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: still some motivating examples worth talking about</p>
<p dir="auto">BF: a point: top-level await is going to pretty much wreck node's sync loading anyways</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: if top-level await turns out to be infeasible, then the conflict here is moot, but still worth talking about</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: my concern is that it makes loading unconditional + evaluating conditional, and i think conditional loading is important too</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: skipping over "lazy eval" claim. will note that in node, which has sync i/o, pushing requires into functions has helped startup perf (likely from deferring the i/o)</p>
<p dir="auto">SP: depends on the complexity of the file you're importing</p>
<p dir="auto">SM: biggest perf problem at FB is parsing and initialization. we load everything and only evaluate the things we need. we can't switch to modules without lazy eval + lazy loading.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: i think it will not interfere with lazy loading.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: if you treat all module identifiers the same, then you don't have to parse the whole file, you just have to tokenize it and look for "import" tokens.</p>
<p dir="auto">EF: i think you grossly underestimate the costs involved.</p>
<p dir="auto">... discussion about tokenizing vs parsing; costs thereof; etc ...</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: in my naive understanding of tokenizing and parsing, you could tokenize, iterate, locate <code>import</code>, locate the possible path - then optimistically parse that path whether it's a module or not.</p>
<p dir="auto">AK: unless you're talking about throwing away all module early errors, you need to know the exports, so you still have to parse. your approach only would allow easier fetching.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: if i'm wrong about the tokenizing trick, it still doesn't matter where the import declarations are</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: i think the point is that if it turns out that conditional import ...</p>
<p dir="auto">SM: what matters is "do you do the fetch if there's a syntax error"</p>
<p dir="auto">CP: Do we need to have a user make a change to allow for this tokenizing optimization?</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: The optimization is orthogonal</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: He means deferring evaluation</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: That seems unsafe to me.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Colocating imports and code should be good long-term for code hygiene because it should keep the things together and help you as you move the code around. And of course you can keep putting them all at the top level if you want.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Further justification: Put an import in a try/catch block  in case you have fallback behavior.</p>
<p dir="auto">SP: But what if it's not the first time? This scares me. You don't get the chance to execute your fallback behavior.</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: Maybe you should remember the throw and rethrow if it failed.</p>
<p dir="auto">CP: You were saying that it should go to the top</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: To the top of the block. It will still throw from within the try block.</p>
<p dir="auto">AK: To me, this looks like a nested module. It seems like it would be a module in the tree which fails.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Another benefit is dead code elimination--you can avoid running the code by importing only when needed, and this then doesn't run the top-level statements.</p>
<p dir="auto">AK: Dead code elimination will be approximate in JavaScript. It couldn't be done by implementations, but could be done by preprocessors which are OK with changing semantics.</p>
<p dir="auto">MM: Is it valid to eliminate the fetch?</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Technically these change semantics, but may be OK for the application.</p>
<p dir="auto">BF: Module resolution may not include fetch; for Node, things will be pre-populated</p>
<p dir="auto">YK: We did a lot to support the web's asynchronous fetching.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Yes, we needed to support browsers. Nested import declarations seem problematic mostly because of the browser and asynchronous fetch.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: I have a grammar in the proposal. Import is not allowed in non-modules. There is a PR, I invite people to participate there. Nested imports from scripts are prohibited with static semantics.</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: We have settled on declarative imports, hoisting them. But nested imports give you a tool for doing imperative imports, though no deferred fetching and specifiers are literal</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: Stage 1?</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: Suggested area of exploration: await import</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="async fn() {
    await import {x} from m;
}"><pre class="notranslate"><code>async fn() {
    await import {x} from m;
}
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">AWB, YK: We should merge this group with the loader group to come back in a few months.</p>
<p dir="auto">AK: I'm surprised you're not upset about it being a promise.</p>
<p dir="auto">DH: But the Promise is not reified</p>
<p dir="auto">BN: I would prefer synchronous semantics, if possible, but we could live with this. For one, it would be nice to run successive import lines in parallel.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h4 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Conclusion/Resolution</h4><a id="user-content-conclusionresolution-9" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Conclusion/Resolution" href="#conclusionresolution-9"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Stage 0 holds.
<ul dir="auto">
<li>A synthesis proposal will be pursued at a future meeting.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>

</react-app>




  </div>

</turbo-frame>

    </main>
